We also have PA^2 = PC^2. PA^2 = (p_x-0)^2 + (p_y - h)^2 = p_x^2 + (p_y-h)^2. PC^2 = (p_x - c)^2 + (p_y-0)^2 = (p_x-c)^2 + p_y^2. Since p_x = (c+d)/2, we have p_x - c = (d-c)/2, and p_x - d = (c-d)/2. So PC^2 = ((d-c)/2)^2 + p_y^2. So equality gives:

p_x^2 + (p_y-h)^2 = ((d-c)/2)^2 + p_y^2

=> p_x^2 + p_y^2 - 2h p_y + h^2 = ((d-c)/2)^2 + p_y^2

Cancel p_y^2: p_x^2 - 2h p_y + h^2 = ((d-c)/2)^2

=> 2h p_y = p_x^2 + h^2 - ((d-c)/2)^2

=> p_y = (p_x^2 + h^2 - ((d-c)/2)^2) / (2h)

Now note that ((d-c)/2)^2 = ( (x_N+R - (x_M - r))/2 )^2 = ( (x_N - x_M + R + r)/2 )^2. And p_x = (x_M + x_N + R - r)/2.

Similarly D1 = (1/2)[ -x_N(c+d) + c d - h^2 ].

From second: y = 1.618(x-1) -1.618 = 1.618x -1.618 -1.618 = 1.618x -3.236. Substitute into first: (1.618x -3.236)+0.7235 = -1.618(x-0.447) => 1.618x -2.5125 = -1.618x + 0.723? Actually -1.618*0.447 = -0.723, so -1.618(x-0.447)= -1.618x+0.723. So equation: 1.618x -2.5125 = -1.618x+0.723 => 3.236x = 3.2355 => x≈1.0. Then y=1.618*1 -3.236 = -1.618. So center O_c = (1.0, -1.618). Radius = distance to B: sqrt((1-0)^2 + (-1.618+1)^2) = sqrt(1 + (-0.618)^2)= sqrt(1+0.382)= sqrt(1.382)=1.176. Now line L: param (1.618(1+s), -0.382 -2.618s). Does it have distance to O_c equal radius? Compute distance from O_c to L. Vector direction v = (1.618, -2.618). Compute a point on L: H=(1.618,-0.382). O_c = (1, -1.618). Compute vector from H to O_c: O_c-H = (1-1.618, -1.618+0.382) = (-0.618, -1.236). Now compute the distance from O_c to line L: |(O_c-H) × v| / |v|. Cross product magnitude in 2D: |(dx,dy) × (vx,vy)| = |dx*vy - dy*vx|. Compute: (-0.618)*(-2.618) - (-1.236)*(1.618) = 1.618 - (-1.236*1.618?) Actually (-1.236)*(1.618)= -2.0? Let's compute precisely: 1.236*1.618 = 1.236*1.618 = 1.236*1.6=1.9776, 1.236*0.018=0.022248, sum=1.999848 ≈2. So (-1.236)*(1.618)= -2.0. Then (-0.618)*(-2.618)= 1.618? 0.618*2.618=1.618 (since 0.618*2.618=1.618). So dx*vy = 1.618, dy*vx = (-1.236)*(1.618)= -2.0, so dx*vy - dy*vx = 1.618 - (-2.0) = 3.618. So |cross| = 3.618. |v| = sqrt(1.618^2+2.618^2)= sqrt(2.618+6.854)= sqrt(9.472)=3.078. So distance = 3.618/3.078 = 1.176, which equals radius. So indeed L is tangent. Good.

So the algebraic approach seems promising. Now we need to prove that for all parameters, the distance from the center of circle (BEF) to line L equals the radius of that circle. We can attempt to compute the center and radius of circle (BEF) in terms of the parameters and then verify the tangency condition.

We have points: B=(0,-h), E=(t_E p_x, h + t_E β), F=(t_F p_x, h + t_F β). And we know t_E = -r K / |v|^2, t_F = -R K / |v|^2, with K = x_M - x_N - r - R. Also, we have p_x = (c+d)/2, β = (c d - h^2)/(2h), and we have relations linking c,d to x_M,x_N,r,R,h. But perhaps we can find expressions for E and F that simplify further.

Since t_E and t_F are multiples of K, we can write t_E = -r K / L, t_F = -R K / L, where L = |v|^2 = PA^2.

Then E = ( -r K / L * p_x, h + (-r K / L) β ) = ( -r K p_x / L, h - r K β / L ). Similarly F = ( -R K p_x / L, h - R K β / L ).

Now, note that L = p_x^2 + β^2.

Now, we have B = (0, -h). So we need to find the circle through these three points. Perhaps we can find that the circumcenter of triangle BEF is simply (p_x, something) or something like that. Let's compute using the fact that E and F lie on line through A with direction v. The line AP has equation: (x,y) = (0,h) + u (p_x, β). So E corresponds to u = t_E, F to u = t_F. So we have coordinates param u.

Now, consider the circle through B and two points on a line. There is a known property: The center of the circle lies on the perpendicular bisector of the chord EF, which is perpendicular to v. Also, the center lies on the perpendicular bisector of BE. We can try to find the center O_c = (x_c, y_c) by solving equations.

Let’s denote:

- For a point X, we have circle equation: (x - x_c)^2 + (y - y_c)^2 = R_c^2.
- For B: (0 - x_c)^2 + (-h - y_c)^2 = R_c^2 => x_c^2 + (h + y_c)^2 = R_c^2. (1)
- For E: (t_E p_x - x_c)^2 + (h + t_E β - y_c)^2 = R_c^2. (2)
- For F: (t_F p_x - x_c)^2 + (h + t_F β - y_c)^2 = R_c^2. (3)

Subtract (1) from (2): (t_E p_x - x_c)^2 + (h + t_E β - y_c)^2 - x_c^2 - (h + y_c)^2 = 0.
Expand: t_E^2 p_x^2 - 2 t_E p_x x_c + x_c^2 + (h + t_E β)^2 - 2 (h + t_E β) y_c + y_c^2 - x_c^2 - (h^2 + 2h y_c + y_c^2) = 0 => t_E^2 p_x^2 - 2 t_E p_x x_c + (h + t_E β)^2 - 2 (h + t_E β) y_c - h^2 - 2h y_c = 0. (since x_c^2 and y_c^2 cancel). So:

t_E^2 p_x^2 - 2 t_E p_x x_c + (h^2 + 2h t_E β + t_E^2 β^2) - 2h y_c - 2 t_E β y_c - h^2 - 2h y_c = 0 => Cancel h^2: t_E^2 p_x^2 - 2 t_E p_x x_c + 2h t_E β + t_E^2 β^2 - 2h y_c - 2 t_E β y_c - 2h y_c = 0 => t_E^2 (p_x^2+β^2) - 2 t_E p_x x_c + 2h t_E β - 4h y_c - 2 t_E β y_c = 0. But note that p_x^2+β^2 = L. So:

t_E^2 L - 2 t_E p_x x_c + 2h t_E β - 4h y_c - 2 t_E β y_c = 0. (A)

Similarly, subtract (1) from (3): t_F^2 L - 2 t_F p_x x_c + 2h t_F β - 4h y_c - 2 t_F β y_c = 0. (B)

Now, we have t_E and t_F known. Since t_E = -r K / L, t_F = -R K / L. Let's denote T = K / L. Then t_E = -r T, t_F = -R T. Note that T is negative? K negative, L positive, so T negative. So t_E and t_F positive? -r T: since r>0, T negative, -r T >0. Good.

Now, substitute into (A): (-r T)^2 L - 2 (-r T) p_x x_c + 2h (-r T) β - 4h y_c - 2 (-r T) β y_c = 0 => r^2 T^2 L + 2r T p_x x_c - 2h r T β - 4h y_c + 2r T β y_c = 0. Since T^2 L = (K^2/L^2) * L = K^2 / L. But we might not need that. Let's keep.

Similarly, (B): R^2 T^2 L + 2R T p_x x_c - 2h R T β - 4h y_c + 2R T β y_c = 0.

Now, note that T = K/L. So we have T^2 L = K^2/L.

Thus the equations become:

r^2 (K^2/L) + 2r (K/L) p_x x_c - 2h r (K/L) β - 4h y_c + 2r (K/L) β y_c = 0. (A')
R^2 (K^2/L) + 2R (K/L) p_x x_c - 2h R (K/L) β - 4h y_c + 2R (K/L) β y_c = 0. (B')

Multiply both equations by L/K (assuming K≠0, which holds because circles intersect at two points, so x_M - x_N - r - R ≠ 0? Let's check: For intersecting circles, the distance between centers d = |x_N - x_M|. And r = sqrt(x_M^2+h^2), R = sqrt(x_N^2+h^2). For circles to intersect, we need d > |R-r| and d < R+r. Since x_M and x_N have opposite signs? Not necessarily. But K = x_M - x_N - r - R. Could be zero? If x_M - x_N = r+R, then since r+R > d? Actually d = |x_N-x_M|, and r+R > d typically. So x_M - x_N could be negative; r+R positive, so x_M - x_N - r - R <0, not zero. Could it be zero? That would require x_M - x_N = r+R. But since r+R > |x_N-x_M|, and x_M - x_N could be negative if x_M < x_N; then left side negative, right side positive, impossible. If x_M > x_N, then x_M - x_N positive; but then r+R > d = x_M-x_N; so x_M - x_N < r+R, so x_M - x_N - r - R <0. So K is negative. So K≠0. So we can divide by K.

Thus we have:

r^2 (K/L) * (L/K)? Actually (A') times L/K: r^2 (K^2/L) * (L/K) = r^2 K. Then 2r (K/L) p_x x_c * (L/K) = 2r p_x x_c. Then -2h r (K/L) β * (L/K) = -2h r β. Then -4h y_c * (L/K) = -4h y_c L/K. Then 2r (K/L) β y_c * (L/K) = 2r β y_c. So (A'') becomes:

r^2 K + 2r p_x x_c - 2h r β - 4h y_c L/K + 2r β y_c = 0. (A'')

Similarly, (B'') becomes:

R^2 K + 2R p_x x_c - 2h R β - 4h y_c L/K + 2R β y_c = 0. (B'')

Now, we can subtract (A'') from (B'') to eliminate terms involving x_c and y_c? Actually both have 2 p_x x_c and -4h y_c L/K, etc. Subtract:

(B'') - (A''): (R^2 - r^2)K + 2(R - r) p_x x_c - 2h (R - r) β + 2(R - r) β y_c = 0 => Divide by (R - r) (which is positive since R>r): (R+r)K + 2 p_x x_c - 2h β + 2 β y_c = 0. (C)

Thus we have:

2 p_x x_c + 2 β y_c = 2h β - (R+r)K. (C')

Now, we also have from (A''):

r^2 K + 2r p_x x_c - 2h r β - 4h y_c L/K + 2r β y_c = 0. We can divide by r (r>0): r K + 2 p_x x_c - 2h β - 4h y_c L/(r K) + 2 β y_c = 0. So:

2 p_x x_c + 2 β y_c = 2h β - r K + 4h y_c L/(r K). (A''')

Similarly, from (B''): R K + 2 p_x x_c - 2h β - 4h y_c L/(R K) + 2 β y_c = 0 => 2 p_x x_c + 2 β y_c = 2h β - R K + 4h y_c L/(R K). (B''')

But we already have (C'): 2 p_x x_c + 2 β y_c = 2h β - (R+r)K. So equate with (A'''): 2h β - (R+r)K = 2h β - r K + 4h y_c L/(r K) => Cancel 2hβ: - (R+r)K = - r K + 4h y_c L/(r K) => Multiply both sides by r K: - (R+r)K * r K = - r K * r K + 4h y_c L => - r (R+r) K^2 = - r^2 K^2 + 4h y_c L => Bring terms: - r (R+r) K^2 + r^2 K^2 = 4h y_c L => r K^2 ( - (R+r) + r ) = 4h y_c L => r K^2 ( -R - r + r ) = 4h y_c L => r K^2 ( -R ) = 4h y_c L => - r R K^2 = 4h y_c L => y_c = - (r R K^2)/(4h L). (1)

Similarly, from (B'''): 2h β - (R+r)K = 2h β - R K + 4h y_c L/(R K) => - (R+r)K = -R K + 4h y_c L/(R K) => Multiply R K: - (R+r)K * R K = -R K * R K + 4h y_c L => - R (R+r) K^2 = -R^2 K^2 + 4h y_c L => -R(R+r)K^2 + R^2 K^2 = 4h y_c L => R K^2 ( - (R+r) + R ) = 4h y_c L => R K^2 ( -r ) = 4h y_c L => -r R K^2 = 4h y_c L => same as before. So consistent.

Thus we have y_c = - (r R K^2)/(4h L). Good.

Now we also have equation (C'): 2 p_x x_c + 2 β y_c = 2h β - (R+r)K. So we can solve for x_c:

2 p_x x_c = 2h β - (R+r)K - 2 β y_c => x_c = [2h β - (R+r)K - 2 β y_c] / (2 p_x).

Now, we have y_c expressed. So the center O_c = (x_c, y_c).

Now, we need to prove that line L through H parallel to v is tangent to circle (BEF). The line L: H + s v, with H = (p_x, y_H). v = (p_x, β). So L is param: (p_x(1+s), y_H + s β). We can compute the distance from O_c to L and show it equals radius R_c = distance from O_c to B.

Let w = O_c - H = (x_c - p_x, y_c - y_H). Then the distance d = |w × v| / |v| = |(x_c - p_x) * β - (y_c - y_H) * p_x| / |v|. Actually for two circles intersecting, the radical axis is the line through the intersection points. So AB is the line through origin and B. So the radical axis is the line through origin and B. So AB is a line through origin. And the line of centers MN is the line through m and n. The condition that AB is perpendicular to MN is a geometric condition. So in our complex representation, we must have that the line through origin and B is perpendicular to the line through m and n. That means that the direction of B (i.e., arg(B)) is perpendicular to the direction of n-m (i.e., arg(n-m)). So arg(B) = arg(n-m) + π/2 mod π. So B is on a line through origin that is perpendicular to MN. That is consistent: AB is perpendicular to MN. So in the complex representation, we can set B = i λ (n-m) for some real λ? Actually if we set the direction of n-m as unit vector u, then u is some complex number of modulus 1. Then the perpendicular direction is i u. So B = t i u for some real t (since B is on line through origin). So B = t i u, where u = (n-m)/|n-m|. So B is a pure imaginary multiple of u. That is a useful normalization: Let u = (n-m)/|n-m|, so u is a unit complex number. Then m and n are such that n-m = d u, where d = |n-m|. And we have |m|=r, |n|=R. Also, we have that B = t i u for some t > 0 (since we can choose orientation). And the other intersection point is B (the second intersection). So we have A=0, B = t i u, with t real.

Now, we also have that C and D are on the line through m and n. Since m and n are on the line, we can express them in terms of u. Let’s denote the real coordinate along u: let the real axis be along u. So we can set u = 1 (by rotation) because we can rotate the whole configuration. So let u = 1 (i.e., the direction of MN is real axis). Then the perpendicular direction is i. So we have that MN is along real axis. Then m and n are real numbers? Since they lie on the line through centers, which we can set as real axis. So m and n are real numbers. But then A=0 is on both circles, so |m|=r, |n|=R. Since m,n real, we have m = ±r, n = ±R. The order C, M, N, D: C is on Ω on the side away from N, so if M is between C and N, then C is to the left of M. That would mean that if m is positive, then C = m - r? Actually if m>0, then the point on Ω to the left of M is at distance r from M in the negative direction, so C = m - r. That is negative if r>m? But m = ±r, so if m = r, then C = r - r = 0 = A? That would degenerate. So m cannot be r; if m = r, then A=0 is on Ω at distance r from M, but also C would be 0, which is A, not allowed. So m must be -r. Then M = -r, and the point on Ω to the left of M is at -r - r = -2r, so C = -2r. And N: if n = R, then D = n+R = 2R, and order: C=-2r, M=-r, N=R, D=2R? That requires -2r < -r < R < 2R, which holds if r>0, R>0, and -r < R? Actually we need -r < R? That is true if R > -r, which is always since R>0. So we can have m = -r, n = R. Then M = -r, N = R. The distance between centers d = R - (-r) = R+r. That is the maximum possible distance for intersecting circles? Actually for circles to intersect, we need d < R+r? Wait d = R+r, then circles are externally tangent? Because if d = R+r, they are tangent externally. But they are supposed to intersect at two distinct points A and B. So we need d < R+r. But here d = R+r exactly if m = -r and n = R. That would make circles tangent, not two intersections. So that's not allowed. So we need to have m and n such that d < R+r. So m and n cannot be at the extremes of the radii along the real axis. In our coordinate, we had A=0, and circles passing through origin. The centers m and n can be anywhere on the plane, but the line of centers is the line through m and n. We can rotate so that this line is real axis. Then m and n are real numbers. The condition that A=0 is on both circles gives |m|=r, |n|=R. So m = ±r, n = ±R. But then the distance d = |n-m|. If m = r, n = R, then d = |R-r|, and circles are tangent internally? Actually if d = |R-r|, they are internally tangent. If m = -r, n = -R, then d = |R-r|, also internally tangent. If m = r, n = -R, then d = R+r, externally tangent. So to have two intersections, we need d between |R-r| and R+r. So if m and n are real, the only possibilities are m = ±r, n = ±R, and d = |R - r| or R+r. So indeed, with real centers, the circles are tangent, not intersecting in two points. So our assumption that the line of centers is real axis and A=0 is on both circles forces the circles to be tangent? Wait, that's because if A=0 lies on both circles, then the distances from the centers to 0 are exactly the radii. So the centers lie on the circles. So the circles are such that the origin is a point on them. The line of centers is some line. The distance between centers is d. For them to intersect at two points, we need d < R+r and d > |R-r|. But if both centers lie on the circles? Actually m and n are centers; they satisfy |m|=r, |n|=R. So the origin is a point on both circles. The distance between centers is d. Is it possible to have d between |R-r| and R+r without the centers being at the extremes? For example, take m = (r,0), n = (R cosθ, R sinθ) with some θ. Then |m|=r, |n|=R. The distance d = sqrt( (r - R cosθ)^2 + (R sinθ)^2 ) = sqrt( r^2 + R^2 - 2rR cosθ ). For two intersections, we need |R-r| < d < R+r. This is possible for many θ. In our complex representation, we can set u = (n-m)/|n-m|. But we can also rotate so that the line through m and n is real axis? If we rotate so that the line through m and n is real axis, then m and n become real numbers. But then we also have that the origin is on both circles, so the distances from the origin to m and n are the radii. So if m and n are real, then |m| = r, |n| = R. So m = ±r, n = ±R. Then d = |n-m| is either |R - r| or R+r. So indeed, if the line of centers is real axis, then the circles are tangent. So to have two intersections, the line of centers cannot be the real axis if we also set A=0? But we rotated so that the line of centers is real axis; that is always possible because we can rotate the whole plane. So if we rotate such that the line of centers is horizontal, then the centers become real numbers. But then the condition that the origin is on both circles forces the distances from the origin to the centers to be the radii. That means that the origin must be on the circles, so the origin's distance to each center equals the radius. So the origin is a point on both circles. But if the centers are on the real axis, then the circles are symmetric about the real axis? Actually the origin is at (0,0). The centers are on the real axis. So the circles have centers on the real axis and pass through the origin. That is possible if the origin is on the circles. For example, take center at (2,0) with radius 2; that circle passes through (0,0). Take another center at (3,0) with radius 3; that circle also passes through (0,0). The distance between centers is 1, and the radii are 2 and 3. Then the circles intersect? The condition for intersection: d=1, |R-r|=1, so they are internally tangent? Actually 3-2=1, so d = |R-r|, so they are internally tangent at the origin? Wait, if two circles have centers on the real axis and both pass through the origin, then the origin is a point of intersection. The distance between centers is d = |n-m|. For them to intersect at another point, we need d > |R-r|. But if both centers are on the real axis and both radii are the distances from the centers to the origin, then the circles are tangent at the origin? Let's check: Circle1: center at (m,0), radius r = |m|. Circle2: center at (n,0), radius R = |n|. If m and n have the same sign, say both positive, then r=m, R=n. The circles both pass through origin. The distance between centers is |n-m|. The condition for two intersections is |n-m| > |R-r| = |n-m|? Actually |R-r| = |n-m|. So d = |R-r|, so the circles are internally tangent at the origin? Because the smaller circle lies inside the larger? But they intersect at origin and possibly another point if d < R+r? For m=2, n=3, we have circles: (x-2)^2+y^2=4, (x-3)^2+y^2=9. Subtract: (x-3)^2 - (x-2)^2 = 5 => (x^2-6x+9)-(x^2-4x+4)= -2x+5=5 => -2x=0 => x=0. So the intersection points satisfy x=0, then (0-2)^2+y^2=4 => 4+y^2=4 => y=0. So only one intersection at (0,0). So indeed they are tangent. If m and n have opposite signs, say m=-2, n=3, then r=2, R=3, distance d=5, which equals R+r, so externally tangent. So indeed, if both centers lie on the same line through the origin (real axis) and the origin is on both circles, then the circles are tangent at the origin. So to have two distinct intersections, the line of centers cannot pass through the origin. In our configuration, A is one of the intersection points, but the line of centers does not necessarily pass through A. So we cannot set A=0 and also have the line of centers be real axis (i.e., through A) because then A would be on the line of centers? Actually we set A=0, and we can rotate so that the line of centers is real axis. That would put the line of centers through the origin? Not necessarily: The line of centers is the line through m and n. If we rotate so that this line is horizontal, then the line is some horizontal line. But we can also translate? Actually we set A=0 by translation. So the origin is at A. Then the line of centers is some line through the plane; we can rotate so that this line is horizontal. That is a rotation about the origin. So after rotation, the line of centers is horizontal, but it may not pass through the origin; it is some horizontal line y = constant? Wait: Rotation about the origin sends the line through m and n to some line through the rotated m and n. But the origin is at A. The line of centers is the line through the centers m and n. If we rotate the whole plane about the origin, then the line through m and n becomes some line through the rotated m and n. Since the rotation is about the origin, the origin remains fixed. So the rotated line of centers will be a line through the rotated points, which may not pass through the origin. Actually if we rotate about the origin, the origin is the rotation center, so any line that does not pass through the origin will be rotated to another line that does not pass through the origin. So we can rotate such that the line of centers becomes horizontal, but it will be some horizontal line y = c, not necessarily y=0. So the centers m and n will have the same y-coordinate c, and their x-coordinates will be something. So we can set m = (m_x, c), n = (n_x, c). And since A=0 is on both circles, we have |m| = r, |n| = R. That is, m_x^2 + c^2 = r^2, n_x^2 + c^2 = R^2. So c is not zero generally. So we can set the line of centers as horizontal line y = c. And then we can translate vertically so that this line becomes the x-axis? But that would move A. So we have a degree of freedom: we can set the line of centers as the x-axis by translating the whole plane vertically, but then A moves off the origin. That's fine. So the earlier coordinate system with A=(0,h) and MN horizontal (x-axis) is general. So we can stick with that.

Given the algebraic complexity, I think the most straightforward rigorous proof is to use coordinate geometry, set up the coordinate system as we did, compute the necessary expressions, and then verify the tangency condition using algebraic manipulations. This is rigorous and can be done with careful algebra. Since the problem is from an olympiad, a synthetic solution may exist, but we can produce an analytic solution. The key is to present the algebra clearly and simplify using the relations.

Let's attempt to do the algebra in a systematic way.

We set coordinates:

- Let M = (0,0), N = (d,0) with d>0.
- Let A = (0, h) with h>0, B = (0, -h). (We can translate horizontally so that A's x-coordinate is 0, because we can choose the perpendicular bisector of AB as the y-axis, and MN is horizontal line? Actually AB is perpendicular to MN, so if we set MN as x-axis, then AB is vertical. We can also set the midpoint of AB as origin, so A=(0,h), B=(0,-h). That's fine; we can translate horizontally to put A at x=0. So we set A at (0,h). Then B=(0,-h). The line MN is horizontal, but not necessarily the x-axis; we can set M and N on some horizontal line y = y0. But we can also translate vertically so that this horizontal line becomes the x-axis? That would move A off the y-axis. So it's better to set MN as x-axis: let M = (m,0), N = (n,0) with m<n (since order C,M,N,D gives M left of N? Actually order C, M, N, D along MN: C left of M, M left of N, N left of D. So M is between C and N, so M is to the left of N? Since C < M < N < D, we have m < n. So we can set M = (m,0), N = (n,0), with m<n. Then A is on both circles. Since A lies on Ω centered M, we have (A_x - m)^2 + (A_y)^2 = r^2. And similarly for Γ: (A_x - n)^2 + (A_y)^2 = R^2. Also, we have that the line MN is the x-axis. The common chord AB is perpendicular to MN, so vertical. So A and B have same x-coordinate. Let that x-coordinate be a. Then A = (a, h), B = (a, -h) for some h>0. So we have a and h. Then from circles: (a-m)^2 + h^2 = r^2, (a-n)^2 + h^2 = R^2.

Now, we can also use the order C, M, N, D: C is the intersection of Ω with line MN on the side away from N. Since MN is x-axis, the points on Ω on x-axis are at distance r from M. The point on the left of M is at x = m - r. So C = (m - r, 0). Similarly, D = (n + R, 0). The order: m - r < m < n < n + R. So that's fine.

Now, P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. A=(a,h), C=(c,0) with c=m-r, D=(d,0) with d=n+R. Since C and D are on x-axis, the circumcenter P has x-coordinate (c+d)/2 = (m-r + n+R)/2. And y-coordinate given by the perpendicular bisector of AC or AD. Let's compute using AC.

Midpoint of AC: ((a+c)/2, h/2). Slope of AC: (0-h)/(c-a) = -h/(c-a). Perpendicular slope: (c-a)/h. Equation of perpendicular bisector: y - h/2 = ((c-a)/h)(x - (a+c)/2). Since P lies on x = (c+d)/2, we can find p_y.

Thus p_x = (c+d)/2 = (m-r + n+R)/2.
p_y = h/2 + ((c-a)/h)(p_x - (a+c)/2) = h/2 + ((c-a)/h)( (c+d)/2 - (a+c)/2 ) = h/2 + ((c-a)/h)( (d - a)/2 ) = h/2 + (c-a)(d-a)/(2h).

So p_y = (h^2 + (c-a)(d-a))/(2h). Good.

Now, v = P - A = (p_x - a, p_y - h). Compute p_x - a = (c+d)/2 - a = (c - a + d - a)/2 = ((c-a)+(d-a))/2. And p_y - h = (h^2 + (c-a)(d-a))/(2h) - h = (h^2 + (c-a)(d-a) - 2h^2)/(2h) = ((c-a)(d-a) - h^2)/(2h). So v = ( ((c-a)+(d-a))/2, ((c-a)(d-a)-h^2)/(2h) ).

Now, note that c = m-r, d = n+R. And we have relations from circles: (a-m)^2 + h^2 = r^2 => (m-a)^2 + h^2 = r^2. Similarly, (n-a)^2 + h^2 = R^2.

We also have that r = sqrt((a-m)^2+h^2) and R = sqrt((a-n)^2+h^2). So we can treat m,n,a,h as variables satisfying these.

Now, we need to compute t_E and t_F. Since A is on both circles, and we have line AP with direction v, the other intersections are given by t = -2 ( (A - O)·v ) / |v|^2 for each circle.

For Ω: O=M=(m,0). So A-M = (a-m, h). For Γ: O=N=(n,0). So A-N = (a-n, h). Then:

t_E = -2 ( (a-m, h)·v ) / |v|^2 = -2 ( (a-m) v_x + h v_y ) / |v|^2.
t_F = -2 ( (a-n, h)·v ) / |v|^2 = -2 ( (a-n) v_x + h v_y ) / |v|^2.

We also have that |v|^2 = v_x^2+v_y^2.

Now, we can compute these dot products.

Let’s denote:

Δ1 = (a-m) v_x + h v_y.
Δ2 = (a-n) v_x + h v_y.

We have v_x = ((c-a)+(d-a))/2, v_y = ((c-a)(d-a)-h^2)/(2h). So:

Δ1 = (a-m) * ((c-a)+(d-a))/2 + h * ((c-a)(d-a)-h^2)/(2h) = (1/2)[ (a-m)((c-a)+(d-a)) + ((c-a)(d-a)-h^2) ].
Similarly, Δ2 = (1/2)[ (a-n)((c-a)+(d-a)) + ((c-a)(d-a)-h^2) ].

Now, note that c = m - r. So c - a = m - r - a = (m - a) - r. Also, d = n + R, so d - a = n + R - a = (n - a) + R.

Thus (c-a)+(d-a) = (m-a)-r + (n-a)+R = (m+n-2a) + (R - r).

Also, (c-a)(d-a) = ((m-a)-r)((n-a)+R) = (m-a)(n-a) + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR.

So Δ1 = (1/2)[ (a-m)((m-a)-r + (n-a)+R) + ((m-a)(n-a) + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR - h^2) ].

Simplify: (a-m) = -(m-a). So (a-m)((m-a)-r + (n-a)+R) = -(m-a)( (m-a)-r + (n-a)+R ) = -(m-a)((m-a)+(n-a)+R-r) = -(m-a)(m+n-2a+R-r).

Thus Δ1 = (1/2)[ -(m-a)(m+n-2a+R-r) + (m-a)(n-a) + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR - h^2 ].

Now, expand -(m-a)(m+n-2a+R-r) = -(m-a)(m-a + n-a + R-r) = -(m-a)(m-a) - (m-a)(n-a) - (m-a)(R-r) = -(m-a)^2 - (m-a)(n-a) - (m-a)(R-r).

Then Δ1 = (1/2)[ -(m-a)^2 - (m-a)(n-a) - (m-a)(R-r) + (m-a)(n-a) + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR - h^2 ].

The terms -(m-a)(n-a) and +(m-a)(n-a) cancel. So:

Δ1 = (1/2)[ -(m-a)^2 - (m-a)(R-r) + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR - h^2 ] = (1/2)[ -(m-a)^2 - (m-a)R + (m-a)r + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR - h^2 ]? Wait - (m-a)(R-r) = - (m-a)R + (m-a)r. So then we have -(m-a)^2 + [ - (m-a)R + (m-a)r ] + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR - h^2. The - (m-a)R and +(m-a)R cancel. So:

Δ1 = (1/2)[ -(m-a)^2 + (m-a)r - (n-a)r - rR - h^2 ].

Now, note that from the circle Ω: (m-a)^2 + h^2 = r^2 => -(m-a)^2 - h^2 = -r^2. So:

Δ1 = (1/2)[ -r^2 + (m-a)r - (n-a)r - rR ] = (1/2)[ r( -r + (m-a) - (n-a) - R ) ] = (1/2)[ r( m-a - n+a - r - R ) ] = (1/2)[ r( m - n - r - R ) ].

Thus Δ1 = (r/2)( m - n - r - R ). So (A-M)·v = Δ1 = (r/2)( m - n - r - R ).

Similarly, Δ2 = (a-n) v_x + h v_y. Compute (a-n) v_x + h v_y = (a-n)*((c-a)+(d-a))/2 + ((c-a)(d-a)-h^2)/2). Because h v_y = h * ((c-a)(d-a)-h^2)/(2h) = ((c-a)(d-a)-h^2)/2. So:

Δ2 = (1/2)[ (a-n)((c-a)+(d-a)) + (c-a)(d-a) - h^2 ].

Now, (a-n) = -(n-a). So (a-n)((c-a)+(d-a)) = -(n-a)((c-a)+(d-a)). And (c-a)+(d-a) = (m-a)-r + (n-a)+R = (m+n-2a)+(R-r). So:

Δ2 = (1/2)[ -(n-a)((m+n-2a)+(R-r)) + ((m-a)(n-a) + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR - h^2 ].

We can try to simplify similarly. Let's denote:

Δ2 = (1/2)[ -(n-a)(m+n-2a) - (n-a)(R-r) + (m-a)(n-a) + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR - h^2 ].

Now, -(n-a)(m+n-2a) = -(n-a)(m-a + n-a) = -(n-a)(m-a) - (n-a)^2.

So:

Δ2 = (1/2)[ -(n-a)(m-a) - (n-a)^2 - (n-a)(R-r) + (m-a)(n-a) + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR - h^2 ].

The terms -(n-a)(m-a) and +(m-a)(n-a) cancel. So:

Δ2 = (1/2)[ - (n-a)^2 - (n-a)(R-r) + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR - h^2 ].

Now, note that from Γ: (n-a)^2 + h^2 = R^2 => -(n-a)^2 - h^2 = -R^2. So:

Δ2 = (1/2)[ -R^2 - (n-a)(R-r) + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR ] = (1/2)[ -R^2 - (n-a)R + (n-a)r + (m-a)R - (n-a)r - rR ] = (1/2)[ -R^2 - (n-a)R + (m-a)R - rR ] = (1/2)[ -R^2 + R( (m-a) - (n-a) - r ) ] = (1/2)[ -R^2 + R( m - a - n + a - r ) ] = (1/2)[ -R^2 + R( m - n - r ) ] = (R/2)( -R + m - n - r ) = (R/2)( m - n - r - R ). So Δ2 = (R/2)( m - n - r - R ).

Thus we have:

(A-M)·v = (r/2) K,
(A-N)·v = (R/2) K,
where K = m - n - r - R.

Now, note that m - n is negative because m < n, so K is negative (since r,R >0). So K < 0.

Now, |v|^2 = v_x^2+v_y^2. We can compute |v|^2 = PA^2. Since P is circumcenter of ACD, and A is on the circle, PA = PC = PD. We have coordinates: P = (p_x, p_y), A = (a,h). So |v|^2 = (p_x - a)^2 + (p_y - h)^2. We can compute directly using formulas. But we might also compute |v|^2 in terms of c,d,h. Since p_x = (c+d)/2, p_y = (h^2 + (c-a)(d-a))/(2h). And v = (p_x - a, p_y - h). So:

v_x = (c+d)/2 - a = (c-a + d-a)/2.
v_y = (h^2 + (c-a)(d-a))/(2h) - h = ((c-a)(d-a) - h^2)/(2h).

So |v|^2 = ((c-a + d-a)^2)/4 + ((c-a)(d-a) - h^2)^2/(4h^2) = (1/4)[ (c-a + d-a)^2 + ((c-a)(d-a) - h^2)^2/h^2 ].

Now, we also have that c = m - r, d = n + R. And we have relations: (m-a)^2 + h^2 = r^2, (n-a)^2 + h^2 = R^2. So we can express c-a = (m-r) - a = (m-a) - r. Similarly, d-a = (n+R) - a = (n-a) + R.

Thus we can compute in terms of m,n,a,h.

Now, we also have t_E = -2 ( (A-M)·v ) / |v|^2 = -2 * (r/2 K) / |v|^2 = -r K / |v|^2.
t_F = -2 ( (A-N)·v ) / |v|^2 = -2 * (R/2 K) / |v|^2 = -R K / |v|^2.

Since K < 0, t_E, t_F > 0.

Thus E = A + t_E v = (a, h) + t_E (v_x, v_y). Similarly F.

Now, we need the circumcircle of BEF, with B = (a, -h). And we need to show that line L: H + s v, where H = (p_x, y_H) with y_H = (p_x - m)(n - p_x)/p_y, is tangent to this circle.

We have already derived general expressions for the circumcenter O_c = (x_c, y_c) in terms of a, h, p_x, p_y, etc., with a=0? Actually we set A=(a,h). In our current coordinate, a is not necessarily 0. But we can use the general formulas we derived earlier that are valid for any a. However, we have a relation that A is on both circles, and we have expressions for v_x, v_y, etc. Maybe we can find that the circle (BEF) has center (p_x, something) and that the line L is the polar of B with respect to that circle.

Let's try to compute O_c using the method we used earlier but with general a. We had derived:

- For points A=(a,h), B=(a,-h), and we have v = (v_x, v_y) with v = P-A.
- We defined t_E = -r K / L, t_F = -R K / L, where L = |v|^2, K = x_M - x_N - r - R? Wait careful: In our earlier derivation with A=(a,h) and M=(x_M,0), N=(x_N,0), we had K = x_M - x_N - r - R? Given the algebraic complexity, perhaps we can find a more clever analytic approach: Use coordinates with A at origin? That is, set A = (0,0) by translation. Then circles Ω and Γ have centers M=(m, c) and N=(n, c) with m<n, and both pass through (0,0) and (0,b). So we have:

For Ω: (0-m)^2 + (0-c)^2 = r^2 => m^2+c^2 = r^2. Also, (0-m)^2 + (b-c)^2 = r^2 => m^2+(b-c)^2 = r^2. Subtract: (b-c)^2 - c^2 = 0 => b^2 - 2bc + c^2 - c^2 = b(b-2c)=0 => Since b>0, we get c = b/2. So the y-coordinate of the centers is b/2. So M=(m, b/2), N=(n, b/2). Also, from the first equation: m^2 + (b/2)^2 = r^2. And from the second: m^2 + (b/2)^2 = r^2 as well, consistent. Now, P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. A=(0,0), C=(m-r, b/2), D=(n+R, b/2). Since C and D are on horizontal line y=b/2, the circumcenter P has x-coordinate ( (m-r)+(n+R) )/2 = (m+n+R-r)/2. We can compute p_y in terms of m,n,b,r,R. Also, v = P - A = (p_x, p_y). So v = (p_x, p_y). And line AP is through A and P. So direction v.

Vector MH = (p_x - m, y_H - b/2). Vector PN = (n - p_x, b/2 - p_y). Perpendicular: (p_x - m)(n - p_x) + (y_H - b/2)(b/2 - p_y) = 0 => y_H = b/2 + (p_x - m)(n - p_x)/(p_y - b/2). But p_y - b/2 =? So y_H = b/2 + (p_x - m)(n - p_x)/(p_y - b/2). That is one expression.

Now, we need to show that line through H parallel to AP (direction v) is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF, where B=(0,b) (since B=(0,b) from earlier? Actually B = (0,b) because we set A=(0,0), B on y-axis. So B=(0,b). And E and F are the second intersections of line AP with circles Ω and Γ. Now, v = (p_x, p_y). So we can compute these.

We also have that P is circumcenter of ACD, so PA = PC = PD. That gives that PA^2 = p_x^2+p_y^2 = PC^2 = (p_x - (m-r))^2 + (p_y - b/2)^2. This is a relation that can be used.

- The radius of Ω: r = distance from M to A = sqrt(m^2 + (b/2)^2). So r = sqrt(m^2 + (b/2)^2).
- The radius of Γ: R = distance from N to A = sqrt(n^2 + (b/2)^2).
- C is the intersection of Ω with line MN (y=b/2) on the side away from N. Since M=(m,b/2), the point on Ω to the left of M is at x = m - r. So C = (m - r, b/2).
- D = (n + R, b/2).
- The order: m - r < m < n < n + R.

Now, P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. A=(0,0), C=(c, b/2) with c = m - r, D=(d, b/2) with d = n + R. And y-coordinate p_y is given by the perpendicular bisector of AC or AD. Compute using AC: Midpoint of A and C is (c/2, b/4). Slope of AC: (b/2 - 0)/(c - 0) = (b/2)/c. Perpendicular slope: -c/(b/2) = -2c/b. Equation of perpendicular bisector: y - b/4 = (-2c/b)(x - c/2). Since P lies on x = p_x, we have p_y = b/4 + (-2c/b)(p_x - c/2) = b/4 - (2c/b)(p_x - c/2).

Now, v = P - A = (p_x, p_y). So line AP is through origin with direction v.

E = (2(v·M)/|v|^2) v,
F = (2(v·N)/|v|^2) v.

Given the time, I think we can produce a proof by using coordinates and then using the fact that the condition reduces to an identity that can be verified by substituting the relations. This is acceptable for a rigorous proof. I will attempt to carry out the algebra.

We have many variables, but we can treat m,n,a,h as independent and then r,R defined. However, we have the constraints that A lies on circles, so r = sqrt((a-m)^2+h^2), R = sqrt((a-n)^2+h^2). That's fine. We can keep r,R as variables and treat them as functions of m,n,a,h.

Now, let's compute some important expressions.

First, compute v_x = p_x - a. We have p_x = (m-r + n+R)/2. So v_x = (m-r + n+R)/2 - a = (m+n+R-r - 2a)/2.

But we also have from the circle equations: (a-m)^2+h^2 = r^2 => a^2 -2am + m^2 + h^2 = r^2 => r^2 = a^2 -2am + m^2 + h^2. Similarly, R^2 = a^2 -2an + n^2 + h^2.

We might also need expressions like m - r and n + R.

Now, compute v_y = p_y - h. p_y = (h^2 + (m-r)(n+R))/(2h). So v_y = (h^2 + (m-r)(n+R))/(2h) - h = (h^2 + (m-r)(n+R) - 2h^2)/(2h) = ((m-r)(n+R) - h^2)/(2h).

Thus v = ( (m+n+R-r-2a)/2, ((m-r)(n+R)-h^2)/(2h) ). 

Now, we also have K = m - n - r - R.

We have L = v_x^2+v_y^2.

Now, we have t_E = -rK/L, t_F = -RK/L. Since K<0, these are positive.

Consider circle (ACD) with center P. Then points A, C, D lie on this circle. The line AP is the line through A and the center, so it is a diameter of the circle? Actually if you have a circle with center P, then any line through P that goes through a point on the circle will have the property that the point is at distance R from P. But the line through A and P will intersect the circle at A and at the point opposite A (the antipode of A on the circle). That antipode is the point A' such that PA' = PA and A, A' are opposite ends of a diameter. So the line AP is a diameter of the circle (ACD) only if A is the antipode of the point where the line meets again? Actually if you have a circle with center P, then for any point X on the circle, the line through P and X is a radius; the opposite point on the circle is the point X' such that P is the midpoint of XX'. So the line through P and X is a diameter only if you take the two points X and X' which are opposite ends of a diameter. So the line AP is a diameter of the circle (ACD) only if A is such that the line through P and A meets the circle again at the antipode of A. But we don't know that. However, we can consider the circle (ACD) and the points E and F. Since E is on Ω and on line AP, and A is on Ω, we have that E is the second intersection of line AP with Ω. So E is not necessarily on circle (ACD). So not.

The Simson line of a point on the circumcircle of a triangle is the line connecting the feet of perpendiculars to the sides. Now, the circle through B and two points on a line through origin can be described. The general equation of a circle through origin is x^2+y^2 + Ax+By=0. But here we have B on y-axis, and the other points are on ray from origin. So the circle (BEF) will not necessarily pass through origin. However, we can consider the circle through B, E, F. Alternatively, we can compute the circumcenter using the fact that the circumcenter of three points can be found by intersecting the perpendicular bisectors. Since we have coordinates, we can compute O_c using the determinant formula. But perhaps we can find a more clever approach: Use the concept of power of a point. For any point X on line through origin with direction P, the power with respect to circle (BEF) is given by the product of distances to the intersection points of the line with the circle. But the line through origin and P intersects the circle at E and F (since E and F are on that line and on the circle). So the power of origin with respect to the circle is OE * OF = (0? Actually origin is not necessarily on the circle. The power of origin is |O_c|^2 - R_c^2. But we have E and F on the circle, so the power of origin is O_c^2 - R_c^2 = (distance from origin to O_c)^2 - R_c^2 = (distance from origin to O_c)^2 - (distance from O_c to B)^2? Actually R_c = distance from O_c to B, but we can also use that the line through origin intersects the circle at E and F, so the power of origin is (distance from origin to E) * (distance from origin to F) = α|P| * β|P|? Actually |E| = α|P|, |F| = β|P|. So power of origin = αβ|P|^2 = αβ L. So we have:

|O_c|^2 - R_c^2 = αβ L.   (A)

Also, since B is on the circle, we have |O_c - B|^2 = R_c^2.

Now, we also have that the line L through H parallel to P is tangent to the circle. The condition that a line with direction P through point H is tangent is equivalent to that the power of H with respect to the circle equals the square of the distance from H to the point of tangency, but we can also use that the distance from O_c to the line equals R_c. Since the line is parallel to P, its direction is P. So the distance from O_c to line L is |(O_c - H) × P| / |P| = R_c. So we need to show:

|(O_c - H) × P| = R_c |P|.   (B)

Now, we have expressions for H, O_c, P, etc. This is still heavy.

Given the time, I think the analytic solution is the way to go. I will attempt to produce a proof using coordinates with A=(0,1), B=(0,-1), M=(m,0), N=(n,0). I will compute the circumcenter O_c of triangle BEF using the determinant formula, and then show that the distance from O_c to line L equals the radius. This is a lot of algebra, but we can use the fact that the expressions simplify using the relations we have. Since we already have a numeric verification, we can attempt to derive the algebraic identity.

Let's set up:

A = (0,1)
B = (0,-1)
M = (m,0)
N = (n,0) with m < n.

r = sqrt(m^2+1)
R = sqrt(n^2+1)

c = m - r = -1/(m+r) (since (m-r)(m+r)=m^2-r^2 = -1)
d = n + R = 1/(R-n) (since (n+R)(n-R)=n^2-R^2=-1 => n-R = -1/(n+R) => d = n+R = 1/(R-n))

Thus p_x = (c+d)/2 = ( -1/(m+r) + 1/(R-n) )/2.
p_y = (1 + c d)/2 = (1 + (-1/(m+r))*(1/(R-n)))/2 = (1 - 1/( (m+r)(R-n) ))/2.

Now, v = P - A = (p_x, p_y-1) = (p_x, (1 - 1/( (m+r)(R-n) )/2 - 1) = (p_x, - (1 + 1/( (m+r)(R-n) ))/2). So v_x = p_x, v_y = - (1 + T)/2, where T = 1/( (m+r)(R-n) ).

Let L = |v|^2 = p_x^2 + (1+T)^2/4.

Now, K = m - n - r - R.

We have t_E = -rK/L, t_F = -RK/L.

Since K is negative, t_E, t_F positive.

Now, E = (0 + t_E v_x, 1 + t_E v_y) = (t_E p_x, 1 - t_E (1+T)/2).
Similarly, F = (t_F p_x, 1 - t_F (1+T)/2).

Now, B = (0,-1).

We need to find O_c = circumcenter of BEF.

Let's denote:

x_B = 0, y_B = -1.
x_E = t_E p_x, y_E = 1 - t_E (1+T)/2.
x_F = t_F p_x, y_F = 1 - t_F (1+T)/2.

Now, compute the circumcenter using the formula:

Let D = 2(x_B(y_E - y_F) + x_E(y_F - y_B) + x_F(y_B - y_E)). Actually the denominator is 2 times the area: D = x_B(y_E-y_F) + x_E(y_F-y_B) + x_F(y_B-y_E). So D = 0*(y_E-y_F) + x_E(y_F - (-1)) + x_F((-1) - y_E) = x_E(y_F+1) + x_F(-1 - y_E) = x_E(y_F+1) - x_F(1+y_E).

Then the circumcenter coordinates:

x_c = ( (x_B^2+y_B^2)(y_E-y_F) + (x_E^2+y_E^2)(y_F-y_B) + (x_F^2+y_F^2)(y_B-y_E) ) / D
y_c = ( (x_B^2+y_B^2)(x_F-x_E) + (x_E^2+y_E^2)(x_B-x_F) + (x_F^2+y_F^2)(x_E-x_B) ) / D

Since x_B=0, y_B=-1, we have x_B^2+y_B^2 = 0+1=1.

Thus:

x_c = ( 1*(y_E-y_F) + (x_E^2+y_E^2)(y_F+1) + (x_F^2+y_F^2)(-1 - y_E) ) / D
y_c = ( 1*(x_F-x_E) + (x_E^2+y_E^2)(0 - x_F) + (x_F^2+y_F^2)(x_E - 0) ) / D = ( (x_F-x_E) - x_F(x_E^2+y_E^2) + x_E(x_F^2+y_F^2) ) / D

Now, D = x_E(y_F+1) - x_F(1+y_E) = x_E(y_F+1) - x_F(1+y_E).

We also have that t_E and t_F are related: t_E = r S/L, t_F = R S/L, where S = 1/(m+r) + 1/(R-n) =? Actually from earlier, we had S = 1/(m+r) + 1/(R-n). And p_x = ( -1/(m+r) + 1/(R-n) )/2 = ( -1/(m+r) + 1/(R-n) )/2. So p_x is not directly proportional to S.

But we can try to express everything in terms of m and n. This is still heavy.

Given the time, I think we should produce a solution that uses coordinates and then verifies the tangency condition by showing that the power of H with respect to the circle (BEF) equals the square of the distance from H to the line through H parallel to AP? Wait, the line L passes through H, so the distance from H to L is 0, so the power of H with respect to the circle is the square of the length of the tangent from H to the circle, which is not directly the distance to the line. However, if L is tangent, then the distance from the center O_c to L equals R_c, and that distance is |(O_c - H) × v|/|v|. So we need to show that |(O_c - H) × v| = R_c |v|.

Since we have expressions for O_c, H, v in terms of m,n, we can attempt to compute this cross product and show it equals R_c |v|. This is still heavy.

Maybe we can find that O_c is the reflection of something across H. In the example, O_c = (1,2.618), H = (1.618,1.382), v = (1.618,2.618). The vector from H to O_c is (-0.618,1.236). The cross product with v: |(-0.618)*2.618 - 1.236*1.618| = 3.618. R_c = 1.176, |v| = 3.078, product = 3.618. So indeed.

Now, note that in this example, O_c = (p_x - (p_y - 1)? Actually p_x = 1.618, p_y = 2.618, so p_y - 1 = 1.618 = p_x. Then O_c = (1, p_y). And H = (p_x, 1 + (p_x - m)(n-p_x)/(p_y-1)?) Not.

Maybe we can derive that O_c = (p_x - (p_y - 1)? Not.

Given the difficulty, I think we need to produce a solution that is more synthetic. Let's try to find an angle chase.

We want to show that line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). That means that the angle between this line and the chord from the point of tangency is 90°. Or that the line makes an angle with the radius equal to 90°. So we need to show that the line L is perpendicular to the radius at the point of tangency. Since we don't know the point of tangency, we can instead show that the line L is the Simson line of some point with respect to triangle BEF. The Simson line of a point on the circumcircle of a triangle is the line connecting the feet of perpendiculars to the sides. If we can show that H is the foot of a perpendicular from some point to some side, and that the Simson line is parallel to AP, then the line through H parallel to AP is that Simson line, and it is known that the Simson line is tangent to the circle if the point is such that? Actually the Simson line is not necessarily tangent; it's just a line.

Maybe we can consider the circle (BEF). Since B is on the circle, and E,F are on line AP, the line AP is a chord. The line through H parallel to AP might be the line that is the reflection of the line through B and something.

Another idea: Use the fact that the orthocenter H of triangle PMN is the center of the circle that is the image of the nine-point circle under some homothety. Not.

Given the time, I think we should produce an analytic solution that is manageable by using computer algebra, but since we are to present a proof, we can present the algebra in a structured way, showing that the condition reduces to an identity that can be verified using the relations. This is acceptable for an olympiad solution if the algebra is simplified. I will try to produce a clean analytic proof.

Let's choose a coordinate system that simplifies the algebra. Set A = (0, 1), B = (0, -1). Let M = (m, 0), N = (n, 0) with m < n. Then r = sqrt(m^2+1), R = sqrt(n^2+1). We have c = m - r = -1/(m+r), d = n+R = 1/(R-n). Then p_x = (c+d)/2, p_y = (1+cd)/2.

Now, v = (p_x, p_y-1). Let’s compute p_y-1 = (1+cd)/2 -1 = (cd-1)/2. So v = (p_x, (cd-1)/2).

Now, note that cd = (-1/(m+r)) * (1/(R-n)) = -1/((m+r)(R-n)). So cd-1 = -(1+1/((m+r)(R-n))). So v_y = - (1+1/((m+r)(R-n)))/2. So v = (p_x, - (1+T)/2) with T = 1/((m+r)(R-n)).

Now, L = |v|^2 = p_x^2 + (1+T)^2/4.

Now, K = m - n - r - R. Since (m-r)(m+r) = -1, we have m-r = -1/(m+r). Similarly, n+R = 1/(R-n). So K = -1/(m+r) - 1/(R-n) = - (1/(m+r) + 1/(R-n)). Let S = 1/(m+r) + 1/(R-n). Then K = -S.

Now, t_E = -rK/L = -r(-S)/L = rS/L. t_F = RS/L.

Thus E = (t_E p_x, 1 + t_E v_y) = (rS p_x/L, 1 - (rS(1+T))/(2L)). Similarly, F = (RS p_x/L, 1 - (RS(1+T))/(2L)).

Now, B = (0, -1).

Now, we can compute the circumcircle of BEF. Let's denote:

α = rS/L, β = RS/L.

Then E = (α p_x, 1 - α(1+T)/2), F = (β p_x, 1 - β(1+T)/2). B = (0, -1).

Now, we can compute the circumcenter O_c. It might be simpler to use the fact that the circle through B and two points on the line through A and P can be described by a formula. Since A = (0,1), the line AP has equation: x/p_x = (y-1)/(v_y) = some parameter. But E and F are on this line, so they satisfy (x, y) = (p_x u, 1 + v_y u) for u = t_E and t_F. So E = (p_x t_E, 1 + v_y t_E), F = (p_x t_F, 1 + v_y t_F). So the points are on the line L_AP: (x, y) = (p_x u, 1 + v_y u). So the line AP is not through origin unless p_x=0, but here p_x may not be zero.

Now, the circle (BEF) passes through B and two points on this line. We can find the equation of this circle using the concept of the circle through a point and two points on a line. There is a standard method: The circle is determined by the chord EF and the point B. The center O_c is the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of EF (which is perpendicular to v) and the perpendicular bisector of BE. We can compute O_c using vector formulas.

Let’s set up vectors:

Let v = (p_x, v_y) as above.

Let’s define u_E = t_E, u_F = t_F. So E = A + u_E v, F = A + u_F v, with A = (0,1). Note that u_E and u_F are positive.

Now, we have B = (0,-1). So we can compute the circumcenter O_c.

We can use the fact that the circumcenter of triangle BEF is given by:

O_c = (|B|^2 (E-F) + |E|^2 (F-B) + |F|^2 (B-E)) / (2 (B×E + E×F + F×B) )? Actually there is a formula using complex numbers. In vector form, the circumcenter is:

O_c = ( (|B|^2 (E-F) + |E|^2 (F-B) + |F|^2 (B-E) ) ) / ( 2 (B × E + E × F + F × B) )? But that's for complex numbers.

Alternatively, we can use the fact that the circumcenter is the point such that the distances to B, E, F are equal. So we have:

|O_c - B|^2 = |O_c - E|^2 = |O_c - F|^2.

From |O_c - B|^2 = |O_c - E|^2, we get:

|O_c|^2 - 2 O_c·B + |B|^2 = |O_c|^2 - 2 O_c·E + |E|^2 => 2 O_c·(E - B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2. (1)
From |O_c - B|^2 = |O_c - F|^2, we get: 2 O_c·(F - B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2. (2)

These are linear equations in O_c. So we can solve for O_c.

We have B = (0,-1), |B|^2 = 1. E = (p_x t_E, 1 + v_y t_E). So |E|^2 = p_x^2 t_E^2 + (1+v_y t_E)^2. Similarly for F.

Let’s denote:

E - B = (p_x t_E, 1+v_y t_E +1) = (p_x t_E, 2+v_y t_E). F - B = (p_x t_F, 2+v_y t_F).

Then equation (1): 2 O_c·(p_x t_E, 2+v_y t_E) = p_x^2 t_E^2 + (1+v_y t_E)^2 - 1 = p_x^2 t_E^2 + 1+2v_y t_E+v_y^2 t_E^2 -1 = (p_x^2+v_y^2) t_E^2 + 2v_y t_E = L t_E^2 + 2v_y t_E.

Similarly, (2): 2 O_c·(p_x t_F, 2+v_y t_F) = L t_F^2 + 2v_y t_F.

Now, note that O_c is the circumcenter. We can write O_c = (X, Y). Then:

2( X p_x t_E + Y (2+v_y t_E) ) = L t_E^2 + 2v_y t_E. (1')
2( X p_x t_F + Y (2+v_y t_F) ) = L t_F^2 + 2v_y t_F. (2')

These are two linear equations in X and Y. We can solve for X and Y.

Let’s denote:

A1 = p_x t_E, B1 = 2+v_y t_E, C1 = L t_E^2 + 2v_y t_E.
A2 = p_x t_F, B2 = 2+v_y t_F, C2 = L t_F^2 + 2v_y t_F.

Then we have:

2( X A1 + Y B1 ) = C1
2( X A2 + Y B2 ) = C2

So:

X A1 + Y B1 = C1/2
X A2 + Y B2 = C2/2

Solve.

Now, we also have H = (p_x, y_H) with y_H = (p_x - m)(n - p_x)/p_y. And we need to show that line L: H + s v is tangent, i.e., distance from O_c to L equals R_c. Since L passes through H and has direction v, the distance is |(O_c - H) × v| / |v| = R_c.

We have R_c = distance from O_c to B = sqrt( (X-0)^2 + (Y+1)^2 ) = sqrt( X^2 + (Y+1)^2 ).

So we need to show:

|(X - p_x, Y - y_H) × (p_x, v_y)| = sqrt( X^2 + (Y+1)^2 ) * sqrt( p_x^2 + v_y^2 ).

Square both sides: ( (X - p_x) v_y - (Y - y_H) p_x )^2 = ( X^2 + (Y+1)^2 )( p_x^2 + v_y^2 ).

This is an identity that must hold given the relations between m,n, and the definitions of t_E, t_F, etc. This is a huge algebraic expression. However, maybe we can find that X and Y have a simple form. Let's try to solve for X and Y from the linear equations.

We have:

X p_x t_E + Y (2+v_y t_E) = (L t_E^2 + 2v_y t_E)/2.
X p_x t_F + Y (2+v_y t_F) = (L t_F^2 + 2v_y t_F)/2.

Let’s denote:

K1 = (L t_E^2 + 2v_y t_E)/2 = (L t_E^2)/2 + v_y t_E.
K2 = (L t_F^2)/2 + v_y t_F.

So:

p_x t_E X + (2+v_y t_E) Y = K1
p_x t_F X + (2+v_y t_F) Y = K2

We can solve for X and Y. This is a 2x2 linear system. The determinant is:

Δ = p_x t_E (2+v_y t_F) - p_x t_F (2+v_y t_E) = p_x [ t_E (2+v_y t_F) - t_F (2+v_y t_E) ] = p_x [ 2(t_E - t_F) + v_y (t_E t_F - t_E t_F) ] = p_x * 2(t_E - t_F). So Δ = 2p_x (t_E - t_F). Since t_E and t_F are different, Δ ≠ 0.

Thus:

X = ( K1 (2+v_y t_F) - K2 (2+v_y t_E) ) / Δ
Y = ( p_x t_E K2 - p_x t_F K1 ) / Δ

Now, we need to compute these in terms of t_E, t_F, p_x, v_y, L. But note that t_E = rS/L, t_F = RS/L. Also, p_x and v_y are given. This is still heavy.

Maybe we can find a relationship that simplifies X and Y. Let's compute K1 and K2.

K1 = (L t_E^2)/2 + v_y t_E.
K2 = (L t_F^2)/2 + v_y t_F.

Then X = ( (L t_E^2/2 + v_y t_E)(2+v_y t_F) - (L t_F^2/2 + v_y t_F)(2+v_y t_E) ) / (2p_x(t_E - t_F)).
Expand numerator:

N_X = (L t_E^2/2)(2+v_y t_F) + v_y t_E(2+v_y t_F) - (L t_F^2/2)(2+v_y t_E) - v_y t_F(2+v_y t_E)
= L t_E^2/2 * 2 + L t_E^2/2 * v_y t_F + 2v_y t_E + v_y^2 t_E t_F - L t_F^2/2 * 2 - L t_F^2/2 * v_y t_E - 2v_y t_F - v_y^2 t_F t_E
= L t_E^2 + (L v_y t_E^2 t_F)/2 + 2v_y t_E + v_y^2 t_E t_F - L t_F^2 - (L v_y t_F^2 t_E)/2 - 2v_y t_F - v_y^2 t_F t_E

The terms (L v_y t_E^2 t_F)/2 and -(L v_y t_F^2 t_E)/2 can be combined: L v_y t_E t_F (t_E - t_F)/2.
The terms v_y^2 t_E t_F cancel: +v_y^2 t_E t_F - v_y^2 t_F t_E = 0.
So:

N_X = L(t_E^2 - t_F^2) + 2v_y(t_E - t_F) + (L v_y t_E t_F (t_E - t_F))/2 = (t_E - t_F)[ L(t_E + t_F) + 2v_y + (L v_y t_E t_F)/2 ].

Thus X = N_X / (2p_x(t_E - t_F)) = [ L(t_E + t_F) + 2v_y + (L v_y t_E t_F)/2 ] / (2p_x).

So X = ( L(t_E + t_F) + 2v_y + (L v_y t_E t_F)/2 ) / (2p_x).   (X)

Now, Y = ( p_x t_E K2 - p_x t_F K1 ) / Δ = p_x ( t_E K2 - t_F K1 ) / (2p_x(t_E - t_F)) = ( t_E K2 - t_F K1 ) / (2(t_E - t_F)).

Compute t_E K2 - t_F K1 = t_E (L t_F^2/2 + v_y t_F) - t_F (L t_E^2/2 + v_y t_E) = (L t_E t_F^2)/2 + v_y t_E t_F - (L t_F t_E^2)/2 - v_y t_F t_E = (L t_E t_F (t_F - t_E))/2 + v_y t_E t_F - v_y t_E t_F = (L t_E t_F (t_F - t_E))/2.

Thus t_E K2 - t_F K1 = (L t_E t_F (t_F - t_E))/2 = - (L t_E t_F (t_E - t_F))/2.

Then Y = ( - (L t_E t_F (t_E - t_F))/2 ) / (2(t_E - t_F)) = - (L t_E t_F) / 4.

So Y = - (L t_E t_F)/4.   (Y)

That is a nice expression! So the y-coordinate of the circumcenter O_c is Y = - (L t_E t_F)/4. And the x-coordinate is given by the formula above.

The distance from O_c=(1, -1.618) to L: we computed cross product = |(1-1.618)*(-2.618) - (-1.618+0.382)*1.618| = |(-0.618)*(-2.618) - (-1.236)*1.618| = |1.618 - (-2.0)| = 3.618. |v| = sqrt(1.618^2+2.618^2)=3.078. So distance = 3.618/3.078 = 1.176. R_c = distance from O_c to B = sqrt( (1-0)^2 + (-1.618+1)^2 ) = sqrt(1 + (-0.618)^2) = sqrt(1+0.382)= sqrt(1.382)=1.176. So tangency holds.

Now, we have O_c = (X, Y) with Y = - (L t_E t_F)/4. And we have H = (p_x, y_H). We need to show that |(X - p_x, Y - y_H) × v| = |v| * sqrt( X^2 + (Y+1)^2 ). This is an identity that should hold given the relations. We can try to compute the cross product and show it equals |v| * R_c. This might simplify using the expressions we have.

Let’s denote:

- p_x = φ = (1+√5)/2 (in the example, but general).

- L = p_x^2+v_y^2.
- t_E = rS/L, t_F = RS/L, with r = sqrt(m^2+1), R = sqrt(n^2+1), S = 1/(m+r)+1/(R-n). But we also have that p_x and v_y are related to m,n via the formulas.

We also have that Y = - (L t_E t_F)/4 = - (L * (rS/L) * (RS/L))/4 = - (r S R S)/(4L) = - (r R S^2)/(4L). So Y = - (r R S^2)/(4L). That is one expression.

Now, we also have that the circumcenter O_c = (X, Y) satisfies that X = ( L(t_E + t_F) + 2v_y + (L v_y t_E t_F)/2 ) / (2p_x). This can be simplified using t_E and t_F.

Alternatively, we can use the following known result: For two circles intersecting at A and B, the circle through the orthocenter of the triangle formed by the centers and the circumcenter of the triangle formed by the intersection points and the point on the line of centers is tangent to the circle through the other intersection and the second intersections of the line through the circumcenter with the circles. Given the time, I think we should present an analytic proof that is rigorous, even if heavy. We can structure it as follows:

- Set up coordinate system with A=(0,1), B=(0,-1). Let M=(m,0), N=(n,0) with m<n.
- Derive expressions for P, v, t_E, t_F, H.
- Compute the circumcenter O_c of triangle BEF using the linear equations, obtaining:
  Y = - (L t_E t_F)/4,
  X = (L(t_E+t_F) + 2v_y + L v_y t_E t_F/2) / (4p_x).
- Show that the line through H parallel to v is tangent to circle (BEF) by verifying that the distance from O_c to that line equals the radius R_c.
- To do this, we can compute the power of H with respect to the circle (BEF) and show that it equals the square of the distance from H to the line? Actually if L is tangent, then the distance from O_c to L equals R_c, which is equivalent to the condition that the equation of L and the circle have a double intersection. We can substitute X = H + s v into the circle equation and show that the resulting quadratic in s has discriminant zero. This discriminant can be expressed in terms of the known quantities and simplified using the relations to zero.

Let's choose the coordinate system with A at the origin. Then we have:

- A = (0,0).
- B = (0,b) with b>0.
- The line of centers MN is horizontal at y = c. Since A and B are on both circles, the centers lie on the perpendicular bisector of AB, which is the horizontal line y = b/2. So c = b/2. So M = (m, b/2), N = (n, b/2) with m < n.
- The radii: r = |M - A| = sqrt(m^2 + (b/2)^2), R = sqrt(n^2 + (b/2)^2).
- The points C and D on MN: C = (m - r, b/2), D = (n + R, b/2). The order: m - r < m < n < n + R.
- P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since A=0, the circumcenter of triangle with vertices 0, C, D is given by the formula: P = (|C|^2 D - |D|^2 C) / (C̅ D - C D̅) in complex numbers. But in vector form, we have:
   |P| = |P - C| = |P - D|.
   Since A=0, the condition |P| = |P - C| gives: |P|^2 = |P|^2 - 2P·C + |C|^2 => 2P·C = |C|^2. So P·C = |C|^2/2. Similarly, P·D = |D|^2/2.
   Also, since C and D are on horizontal line y=b/2, we have C = (c_x, b/2), D = (d_x, b/2). Then P = (p_x, p_y). Then:
   p_x c_x + p_y (b/2) = (c_x^2 + (b/2)^2)/2.
   p_x d_x + p_y (b/2) = (d_x^2 + (b/2)^2)/2.
   Subtract: p_x (c_x - d_x) = (c_x^2 - d_x^2)/2 = (c_x - d_x)(c_x + d_x)/2 => if c_x≠d_x, then p_x = (c_x + d_x)/2.
   So p_x = (c_x + d_x)/2.
   Then from first equation: p_x c_x + p_y b/2 = (c_x^2 + (b/2)^2)/2 => substitute p_x = (c_x+d_x)/2: ((c_x+d_x)/2)*c_x + p_y b/2 = (c_x^2 + b^2/4)/2 => Multiply 2: c_x(c_x+d_x) + p_y b = c_x^2 + b^2/4 => c_x^2 + c_x d_x + p_y b = c_x^2 + b^2/4 => c_x d_x + p_y b = b^2/4 => p_y = b/4 - (c_x d_x)/b.
   Thus P = (p_x, p_y) = ((c_x+d_x)/2, b/4 - (c_x d_x)/b).
   Now, we also have that c_x = m - r, d_x = n + R.
   So p_x = (m - r + n + R)/2 = (m+n+R-r)/2.
   p_y = b/4 - ( (m-r)(n+R) )/b.

- The line AP: through A=0 and P, so direction v = P = (p_x, p_y). So v = P.

- The second intersections of line AP with circles Ω and Γ: Since A=0 is on both circles, the other intersections are given by t v where t satisfies |t v - O| = ρ. For Ω: O=M=(m, b/2), ρ=r. Then |t v - M|^2 = r^2 => t^2 |v|^2 - 2t v·M + |M|^2 = r^2. Since |M|^2 = m^2+(b/2)^2 = r^2, we get t^2 |v|^2 - 2t v·M = 0 => t = 0 or t = 2 v·M / |v|^2. So E = (2 v·M / |v|^2) v.
   Similarly, F = (2 v·N / |v|^2) v.
   Let L = |v|^2 = p_x^2+p_y^2.
   Then t_E = 2 (v·M)/L, t_F = 2 (v·N)/L.
   So E = t_E v, F = t_F v.

- Now, B = (0,b). So triangle BEF: B, E, F are points. The circumcenter O_c of triangle BEF can be computed using linear equations. We derived earlier that Y = - (L t_E t_F)/4. But careful: In this coordinate system, B is (0,b), not (0,-1). So we need to recompute O_c using the linear equations. We can treat the general case.

We have B=(0,b), E=(t_E p_x, t_E p_y), F=(t_F p_x, t_F p_y). And v=(p_x,p_y). We can find O_c by solving:

|O_c - B|^2 = |O_c - E|^2 = |O_c - F|^2.

From |O_c - B|^2 = |O_c - E|^2, we get: 2 O_c·(E - B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2.
From |O_c - B|^2 = |O_c - F|^2, we get: 2 O_c·(F - B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2.

Let’s compute these.

First, |B|^2 = b^2.
|E|^2 = t_E^2 |v|^2 = t_E^2 L.
|F|^2 = t_F^2 L.

E - B = (t_E p_x, t_E p_y - b). F - B = (t_F p_x, t_F p_y - b).

So equations:
2 (X t_E p_x + Y (t_E p_y - b)) = t_E^2 L - b^2.   (1)
2 (X t_F p_x + Y (t_F p_y - b)) = t_F^2 L - b^2.   (2)

We can solve for X and Y.

Now, note that t_E and t_F are positive. Also, we have that v·M = p_x m + p_y (b/2). And v·N = p_x n + p_y (b/2). So t_E = 2 (p_x m + p_y b/2)/L = 2(p_x m + (b/2)p_y)/L. Similarly, t_F = 2(p_x n + (b/2)p_y)/L.

Thus t_E p_x = 2(p_x^2 m + p_x (b/2)p_y)/L. t_E p_y = 2(p_x p_y m + (b/2)p_y^2)/L.

Alternatively, we can use the formula for the circumcenter of three points in terms of determinants. There is known formula: For points A, B, C, the circumcenter is given by ( (|A|^2 (B-C) + |B|^2 (C-A) + |C|^2 (A-B) ) / (2 (A × B + B × C + C × A) ) in complex numbers. In vector form, the circumcenter O_c satisfies:

O_c = ( (|B|^2 (E-F) + |E|^2 (F-B) + |F|^2 (B-E) ) ) / ( 2 (B × E + E × F + F × B) )? Actually in 2D, the formula using coordinates: The circumcenter is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors, which can be found by solving linear equations. X p_x t_E + Y (2+v_y t_E) = (L t_E^2 + 2v_y t_E)/2
X p_x t_F + Y (2+v_y t_F) = (L t_F^2 + 2v_y t_F)/2

Let’s denote:

A2 = t_F p_x, B2 = t_F p_y - b, C2 = (t_F^2 L - b^2)/2.

Then equations:
X A1 + Y B1 = C1
X A2 + Y B2 = C2

We can solve for Y. The determinant Δ = A1 B2 - A2 B1 = t_E p_x (t_F p_y - b) - t_F p_x (t_E p_y - b) = t_E t_F p_x p_y - t_E p_x b - t_F t_E p_x p_y + t_F p_x b = t_F p_x b - t_E p_x b = p_x b (t_F - t_E). So Δ = p_x b (t_F - t_E).

Then Y = ( A1 C2 - A2 C1 ) / Δ = ( t_E p_x C2 - t_F p_x C1 ) / (p_x b (t_F - t_E)) = ( t_E C2 - t_F C1 ) / (b (t_F - t_E)).

Now, C1 = (t_E^2 L - b^2)/2, C2 = (t_F^2 L - b^2)/2.
Thus t_E C2 - t_F C1 = t_E (t_F^2 L - b^2)/2 - t_F (t_E^2 L - b^2)/2 = (1/2)[ t_E t_F^2 L - t_E b^2 - t_F t_E^2 L + t_F b^2 ] = (1/2)[ t_E t_F L (t_F - t_E) + b^2 (t_F - t_E) ] = (1/2)(t_F - t_E)( t_E t_F L + b^2 ).

Therefore, Y = (1/2)(t_F - t_E)( t_E t_F L + b^2 ) / (b (t_F - t_E)) = ( t_E t_F L + b^2 )/(2b).

So Y = (L t_E t_F + b^2)/(2b). That is a nice expression. For the case B=(0,-1), b=-1, then Y = (L t_E t_F + 1)/( -2) = - (L t_E t_F+1)/2. But earlier we had Y = - (L t_E t_F)/4. Let's outline the solution:

1. Set up coordinate system: Let A = (0,0). Let the line AB be the y-axis, so B = (0,b) for some b>0. Since M and N are centers of circles through A and B, they lie on the perpendicular bisector of AB, which is the horizontal line y = b/2. So let M = (m, b/2), N = (n, b/2) with m<n.

2. Compute radii: r = |M-A| = sqrt(m^2 + (b/2)^2), R = |N-A| = sqrt(n^2 + (b/2)^2). The points C and D on MN are: C = (m - r, b/2), D = (n + R, b/2). The order C,M,N,D is satisfied.

3. P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since A=0, we have |P| = |P-C| = |P-D|. This gives P·C = |C|^2/2, P·D = |D|^2/2. Using C=(c_x, b/2), D=(d_x, b/2), we solve: p_x = (c_x+d_x)/2, p_y = b/4 - (c_x d_x)/b. So P = (p_x, p_y) with p_x = (m - r + n + R)/2, p_y = b/4 - ((m-r)(n+R))/b.

4. Line AP: through origin and P, so direction v = P = (p_x, p_y). Let L = |v|^2 = p_x^2 + p_y^2.

5. The second intersections of AP with circles Ω and Γ: For Ω, line through origin with direction v meets Ω again at E = t_E v where t_E = 2(v·M)/L = 2(p_x m + p_y b/2)/L. Similarly, F = t_F v with t_F = 2(p_x n + p_y b/2)/L.

6. Define α = t_E, β = t_F. Then E = (α p_x, α p_y), F = (β p_x, β p_y). B = (0,b).

7. Find circumcenter O_c of triangle BEF. Using the equal distance conditions, we solve linear equations:

   From |O_c - B|^2 = |O_c - E|^2: 2O_c·(E - B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2 = α^2 L - b^2.
   From |O_c - B|^2 = |O_c - F|^2: 2O_c·(F - B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2 = β^2 L - b^2.

   Let O_c = (X,Y). Then we have:

   2( X α p_x + Y (α p_y - b) ) = α^2 L - b^2,
   2( X β p_x + Y (β p_y - b) ) = β^2 L - b^2.

   Solving yields:

   Y = (α β L + b^2)/(2b),   (1)
   X = ( L b (α + β) - L α β p_y + b^2 p_y )/(2 p_x b).   (2)

   (These formulas are derived by elimination.)

8. The orthocenter H of triangle PMN: Since MN is horizontal, the altitude from P is vertical line x = p_x, so H lies on x = p_x. Also, from the condition that MH ⟂ PN, we compute:

   H = (p_x, y_H) where y_H = b/2 + (p_x - m)(n - p_x)/(p_y - b/2).

9. The line L through H parallel to AP: direction v = (p_x, p_y). So its equation is X(s) = (p_x + s p_x, y_H + s p_y).

10. Now we show that this line is tangent to circle (BEF). To do this, compute the distance from O_c to the line L. Since L is parallel to v, the distance is |(O_c - H) × v|/|v|. We compute the cross product:

    (O_c - H) × v = (X - p_x) p_y - (Y - y_H) p_x.

    We need to show that this equals |v| * R_c, where R_c = distance from O_c to B = sqrt(X^2 + (Y - b)^2).

    So we need to verify that:

    ((X - p_x) p_y - (Y - y_H) p_x)^2 = (p_x^2 + p_y^2)(X^2 + (Y - b)^2).

    Substituting the expressions for X and Y in terms of α,β, and using the relations between α,β, p_x, p_y, b, m, n, we can simplify the left side to the right side. This verification is straightforward but lengthy; we can present it by noting that after substitution, the identity reduces to an algebraic identity that holds because of the definitions of α,β and the relationships between p_x, p_y, m, n, b, r, R.

    We can present the verification in a concise way by using the fact that the circumcenter O_c of triangle BEF is given by (X,Y) as above, and that H is the orthocenter of triangle PMN. Then compute the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) and show that it equals the square of the length of the tangent from H to the circle, which is also equal to the square of the distance from H to the point of tangency. But we need to show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent, which is equivalent to that the distance from O_c to that line equals R_c. This can be proven by showing that the equation of the circle and the line have a double intersection. Instead of doing the heavy algebra, we can use a known lemma: For any point H, the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of BEF if and only if H lies on the polar of the point at infinity in the direction of AP with respect to that circle. That is not helpful.

We also have X = ( L b (α+β) - L αβ p_y + b^2 p_y )/(2 p_x b) = ( L b * 2(p_x(m+n)+b p_y)/L - L * (4(p_x m + (b/2)p_y)(p_x n + (b/2)p_y)/L^2) * p_y + b^2 p_y )/(2 p_x b) = ( 2b(p_x(m+n)+b p_y) - 4(p_x m + (b/2)p_y)(p_x n + (b/2)p_y) p_y / L + b^2 p_y )/(2 p_x b).

That is messy.

Let's adopt that.

Set:

A = (0,1)
B = (0,-1)
M = (m,0)
N = (m+1,0) with m real.

Then r = sqrt(m^2+1), R = sqrt((m+1)^2+1) = sqrt(m^2+2m+2).

C = (m - r, 0), D = (m+1 + R, 0). Order: m - r < m < m+1 < m+1+R.

Now, compute P = circumcenter of triangle ACD. A=(0,1), C=(c,0) with c = m - r, D=(d,0) with d = m+1+R. Then p_x = (c+d)/2 = (m - r + m+1+R)/2 = (2m+1+R - r)/2. p_y = (1 + c d)/2 = (1 + (m-r)(m+1+R))/2.

Now, v = P - A = (p_x, p_y-1). So v = (p_x, (1+cd)/2 -1) = (p_x, (cd-1)/2). So v_x = p_x, v_y = (cd-1)/2.

Now, L = |v|^2 = p_x^2 + ((cd-1)/2)^2.

Now, compute t_E = -rK/L, t_F = -RK/L, with K = m - n - r - R = m - (m+1) - r - R = -1 - r - R. So K = -(r+R+1). Then t_E = -rK/L = r(r+R+1)/L. t_F = R(r+R+1)/L.

Thus E = A + t_E v = (t_E p_x, 1 + t_E v_y). F = (t_F p_x, 1 + t_F v_y). B = (0,-1).

Now, we have a one-parameter family m. This seems more tractable. We can compute the circumcenter O_c of triangle BEF using the linear equations. Let's attempt to solve for O_c in terms of m.

Define:

α = t_E, β = t_F.

Then E = (α p_x, 1+α v_y), F = (β p_x, 1+β v_y). B = (0,-1).

We can compute O_c by solving:

|O_c-B|^2 = |O_c-E|^2 => 2 O_c·(E-B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2.
|O_c-B|^2 = |O_c-F|^2 => 2 O_c·(F-B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2.

Let O_c = (X,Y). Then:

E-B = (α p_x, 1+α v_y +1) = (α p_x, 2+α v_y). So equation: 2( X α p_x + Y (2+α v_y) ) = α^2 L - 1? Since |B|^2 = 0^2+(-1)^2=1. And |E|^2 = α^2 (p_x^2+v_y^2) = α^2 L. So RHS = α^2 L - 1.

Similarly, F-B = (β p_x, 2+β v_y), and RHS = β^2 L - 1.

So we have:

2( X α p_x + Y (2+α v_y) ) = α^2 L - 1,   (1)
2( X β p_x + Y (2+β v_y) ) = β^2 L - 1.   (2)

We can solve for X and Y.

Let's denote:

A1 = α p_x, B1 = 2+α v_y, C1 = (α^2 L - 1)/2.
A2 = β p_x, B2 = 2+β v_y, C2 = (β^2 L - 1)/2.

Then we have:

X A1 + Y B1 = C1
X A2 + Y B2 = C2

We can solve using Cramer's rule.

Determinant Δ = A1 B2 - A2 B1 = α p_x (2+β v_y) - β p_x (2+α v_y) = p_x ( 2α + αβ v_y - 2β - αβ v_y ) = p_x ( 2α - 2β ) = 2p_x (α - β). So Δ = 2p_x (α - β).

Now, Y = ( A1 C2 - A2 C1 ) / Δ = ( α p_x C2 - β p_x C1 ) / (2p_x (α-β)) = ( α C2 - β C1 ) / (2(α-β)).

Compute α C2 - β C1 = α (β^2 L - 1)/2 - β (α^2 L - 1)/2 = (1/2)[ α β^2 L - α - β α^2 L + β ] = (1/2)[ αβ L (β - α) + (β - α) ] = (1/2)(β - α)(αβ L + 1). So α C2 - β C1 = (1/2)(β - α)(αβ L + 1). Then Y = [ (1/2)(β - α)(αβ L + 1) ] / [2(α-β)] = (1/2)(β - α)(αβ L+1) / (2(α-β)) = (1/2)(- (α-β))(αβ L+1) / (2(α-β)) = - (αβ L+1)/4.

So Y = - (αβ L + 1)/4.

Similarly, X = ( C1 B2 - C2 B1 ) / Δ = ( C1 (2+β v_y) - C2 (2+α v_y) ) / (2p_x (α-β)). Compute numerator:

N_X = (α^2 L - 1)/2 * (2+β v_y) - (β^2 L - 1)/2 * (2+α v_y) = (1/2)[ (α^2 L - 1)(2+β v_y) - (β^2 L - 1)(2+α v_y) ] = (1/2)[ 2α^2 L + α^2 L β v_y - 2 - β v_y - 2β^2 L - β^2 L α v_y + 2 + α v_y ] = (1/2)[ 2α^2 L - 2β^2 L + α^2 L β v_y - β^2 L α v_y + (α v_y - β v_y) ] = (1/2)[ 2L(α^2 - β^2) + αβ L v_y (α - β) + v_y (α - β) ] = (1/2)(α-β)[ 2L(α+β) + αβ L v_y + v_y ].

Thus N_X = (1/2)(α-β) v_y [ 2L(α+β) + αβ L + 1? Actually careful: (α-β) factor: 2L(α+β) + αβ L v_y? Wait we have 2L(α^2 - β^2) = 2L(α-β)(α+β). So that's (α-β)*2L(α+β). Then we have αβ L v_y (α-β). Then we have v_y (α-β). So N_X = (1/2)(α-β)[ 2L(α+β) + αβ L v_y + v_y ] = (1/2)(α-β) v_y [ 2L(α+β) + αβ L + 1 ]? Actually v_y is a factor? No, the v_y is inside the brackets: 2L(α+β) + αβ L v_y + v_y = 2L(α+β) + v_y(αβ L+1). So N_X = (1/2)(α-β)[ 2L(α+β) + v_y(αβ L+1) ].

Thus X = N_X / (2p_x (α-β)) = (1/2)[ 2L(α+β) + v_y(αβ L+1) ] / (2p_x) = [ 2L(α+β) + v_y(αβ L+1) ] / (4p_x).

So X = ( L(α+β) + (v_y/2)(αβ L+1) ) / (2p_x).

Now, we have Y = - (αβ L+1)/4.

Now, note that α = t_E = r(r+R+1)/L, β = R(r+R+1)/L. So αβ = rR(r+R+1)^2/L^2, and α+β = (r+R)(r+R+1)/L.

Also, v_y = (cd-1)/2 =? But we might also express v_y in terms of m and the parameters. However, we also have p_x = (c+d)/2 = (2m+1+R-r)/2.

Also, from condition that MH ⟂ PN, we have y_H = (p_x - m)(m+1 - p_x)/(p_y - 0)? Actually earlier we had y_H = (p_x - m)(n - p_x)/p_y, with n=m+1. So y_H = (p_x - m)(m+1 - p_x)/p_y.

Idea: Use analytic geometry with coordinates. Let's set up:

Let M = (0,0), N = (d,0) with d>0. Let r = radius of Ω, R = radius of Γ, with r < R. Let A = (a, h) where a = x0 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d) and h = sqrt(r^2 - a^2) > 0. Then B = (a, -h).

C = (-r, 0), D = (d+R, 0). Then we compute P = circumcenter of A, C, D.

Let's compute circumcenter of three points. The perpendicular bisectors.

We can find the circumcenter by solving for point P = (p_x, p_y) such that distances to A, C, D are equal.

Set PA^2 = PC^2 = PD^2.

PC^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2.
PD^2 = (p_x - (d+R))^2 + p_y^2.
PA^2 = (p_x - a)^2 + (p_y - h)^2.

From PC^2 = PD^2: (p_x + r)^2 = (p_x - (d+R))^2 => Expand: p_x^2 + 2r p_x + r^2 = p_x^2 - 2(d+R)p_x + (d+R)^2 => 2r p_x + r^2 = -2(d+R)p_x + (d+R)^2 => 2r p_x + 2(d+R)p_x = (d+R)^2 - r^2 => 2p_x(r + d + R) = (d+R)^2 - r^2 => p_x = [(d+R)^2 - r^2] / [2(r + d + R)].

Simplify numerator: (d+R)^2 - r^2 = d^2 + 2dR + R^2 - r^2. Denominator: 2(r+d+R). So p_x = (d^2 + 2dR + R^2 - r^2) / (2(r+d+R)). We can also write p_x in terms of a? Note that a = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). So d^2 + r^2 - R^2 = 2ad. Then d^2 + 2dR + R^2 - r^2 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) + 2dR + 2R^2 - 2r^2? That seems messy. Let's keep.

Now from PC^2 = PA^2: (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2 = (p_x - a)^2 + (p_y - h)^2 => Expand: p_x^2 + 2r p_x + r^2 + p_y^2 = p_x^2 - 2a p_x + a^2 + p_y^2 - 2h p_y + h^2 => Cancel p_x^2, p_y^2: 2r p_x + r^2 = -2a p_x + a^2 - 2h p_y + h^2 => Bring terms: 2r p_x + 2a p_x + 2h p_y = a^2 + h^2 - r^2 => 2p_x(r+a) + 2h p_y = a^2 + h^2 - r^2. But note that A lies on Ω: a^2+h^2 = r^2. So a^2+h^2 - r^2 = 0. So the right side is 0. So we get: 2p_x(r+a) + 2h p_y = 0 => p_x(r+a) + h p_y = 0 => p_y = - p_x (r+a)/h.

Thus P = (p_x, p_y) with p_y = - p_x (r+a)/h.

Now p_x = (d^2 + 2dR + R^2 - r^2) / (2(r+d+R)). We can also compute r+a? a = (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d). So r+a = r + (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d) = (2dr + d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d) = (d^2+2dr+r^2 - R^2)/(2d) = ((d+r)^2 - R^2)/(2d). So r+a = ((d+r)^2 - R^2)/(2d). Also note that p_x = (d^2+2dR+R^2 - r^2)/(2(r+d+R)). Could simplify using some relations. Also h = sqrt(r^2 - a^2). We might not need explicit p_x; we can perhaps find a geometric relationship.

Now line AP: parametric: A + t*(P-A). We'll find intersection with Ω: x^2+y^2=r^2. Substitute param.

Let vector v = P-A = (p_x - a, p_y - h). Then param: (x,y) = (a, h) + t*(p_x - a, p_y - h). For t=0 we get A; for t=1 we get P. Thus we can compute t_E and t_F.

Let's do general: For circle with center O and radius ρ, and point A on circle, line through A with direction v. The second intersection point is given by t = -2 * ( (A-O)·v ) / (v·v). Since the equation: |A+tv - O|^2 = ρ^2 => |A-O|^2 + 2t (A-O)·v + t^2 |v|^2 = ρ^2. Since |A-O|^2=ρ^2, we get 2t (A-O)·v + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 => t(2(A-O)·v + t|v|^2)=0 => t=0 or t = -2 (A-O)·v / |v|^2. So indeed.

Thus:

For Ω: O=M=(0,0). So (A-M)·v = (a, h)·(p_x-a, p_y-h) = a(p_x-a)+h(p_y-h). And |v|^2 = (p_x-a)^2+(p_y-h)^2. So t_E = -2 [a(p_x-a)+h(p_y-h)] / |v|^2.

Similarly for Γ: O=N=(d,0). So (A-N)·v = (a-d, h)·(p_x-a, p_y-h) = (a-d)(p_x-a)+h(p_y-h). Then t_F = -2 [(a-d)(p_x-a)+h(p_y-h)] / |v|^2.

Thus E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v.

Alternatively, we could use coordinates and simplify using algebraic identities. Let's attempt that, but carefully.

Set up coordinate system as before: M=(0,0), N=(d,0), C=(-r,0), D=(d+R,0). Let A=(a,h) with h>0, a given by formula, h^2 = r^2 - a^2.

We have t = (d+R-r)/2. And p_y = -t*(r+a)/h.

Now compute v = (t-a, p_y-h). Let's compute t-a:

t - a = (d+R-r)/2 - a = (d+R-r - 2a)/2. But a = (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d). So 2a = (d^2+r^2-R^2)/d. Then t-a = (d+R-r)/2 - (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d) = (1/2)[ (d+R-r) - (d^2+r^2-R^2)/d ] = (1/2)[ (d(d+R-r) - (d^2+r^2-R^2)) / d ] = (1/2)[ (d^2+ dR - dr - d^2 - r^2 + R^2) / d ] = (1/2)[ (dR - dr - r^2 + R^2) / d ] = (1/2)[ (d(R-r) + (R^2 - r^2)) / d ] = (1/2)[ (d(R-r) + (R-r)(R+r)) / d ] = (1/2)[ (R-r)(d+R+r) / d ] = ((R-r)(d+R+r))/(2d).

So t - a = ((R-r)(d+R+r))/(2d). That's nice.

Now p_y - h: p_y = -t*(r+a)/h. So p_y - h = -(t(r+a)/h) - h = -(t(r+a)+h^2)/h = -(t(r+a)+r^2 - a^2)/h because h^2 = r^2 - a^2. So p_y - h = -(t(r+a) + r^2 - a^2)/h = -(t(r+a) + (r-a)(r+a))/h = -(r+a)(t + r - a)/h = -(r+a)(t + r - a)/h.

Now compute t + r - a: t = (d+R-r)/2, so t + r - a = (d+R-r)/2 + r - a = (d+R-r + 2r - 2a)/2 = (d+R+r - 2a)/2 = (d+R+r - 2a)/2. And 2a = (d^2+r^2-R^2)/d. So t+r-a = (1/2)[ d+R+r - (d^2+r^2-R^2)/d ] = (1/2)[ (d(d+R+r) - (d^2+r^2-R^2))/d ] = (1/2)[ (d^2+ dR+ dr - d^2 - r^2 + R^2)/d ] = (1/2)[ (dR+ dr - r^2 + R^2)/d ] = (1/2)[ (d(R+r) + (R^2 - r^2))/d ] = (1/2)[ (d(R+r) + (R-r)(R+r))/d ] = (1/2)[ (R+r)(d+R-r)/d ] = ((R+r)(d+R-r))/(2d). So t+r-a = ((R+r)(d+R-r))/(2d). Then p_y - h = -(r+a)*((R+r)(d+R-r))/(2d) / h = -(r+a)(R+r)(d+R-r)/(2dh). But note that t = (d+R-r)/2, so (d+R-r) = 2t. So p_y - h = -(r+a)(R+r)(2t)/(2dh) = -(r+a)(R+r)t/(dh). And t = (d+R-r)/2. So p_y - h = -t(r+a)(R+r)/(dh). That's one expression.

We also have p_y = -t(r+a)/h, so p_y - h = -t(r+a)/h - h = -(t(r+a)+h^2)/h = -(t(r+a)+r^2-a^2)/h = -(t(r+a)+(r-a)(r+a))/h = -(r+a)(t+r-a)/h. And we computed t+r-a = ((R+r)(d+R-r))/(2d) = ((R+r)(2t))/(2d) = t(R+r)/d. So then p_y - h = -(r+a)* (t(R+r)/d)/h = -t(r+a)(R+r)/(dh). Same. Good.

We also have PA^2 = PC^2. PA^2 = (p_x-0)^2 + (p_y - h)^2 = p_x^2 + (p_y-h)^2. PC^2 = (p_x - c)^2 + (p_y-0)^2 = (p_x-c)^2 + p_y^2. Since p_x = (c+d)/2, we have p_x - c = (d-c)/2, and p_x - d = (c-d)/2. So PC^2 = ((d-c)/2)^2 + p_y^2. So equality gives:

p_x^2 + (p_y-h)^2 = ((d-c)/2)^2 + p_y^2

=> p_x^2 + p_y^2 - 2h p_y + h^2 = ((d-c)/2)^2 + p_y^2

Cancel p_y^2: p_x^2 - 2h p_y + h^2 = ((d-c)/2)^2

=> 2h p_y = p_x^2 + h^2 - ((d-c)/2)^2

=> p_y = (p_x^2 + h^2 - ((d-c)/2)^2) / (2h)

Now note that ((d-c)/2)^2 = ( (x_N+R - (x_M - r))/2 )^2 = ( (x_N - x_M + R + r)/2 )^2. And p_x = (x_M + x_N + R - r)/2.

Now note that -x_M^2 - h^2 = -r^2? Since r^2 = x_M^2+h^2, so -x_M^2 - h^2 = -r^2. So D0 = (1/2)[ -r^2 + x_M r - x_N r - r R ] = (1/2)[ r(-r + x_M - x_N - R) ] = (1/2) r ( x_M - x_N - r - R ).

Thus D0 = (r/2)( x_M - x_N - r - R ). Nice!

Similarly, D1 = (1/2)[ -x_N(c+d) + c d - h^2 ]. Compute -x_N(c+d) = -x_N(x_M + x_N + R - r) = -x_M x_N - x_N^2 - x_N(R - r). Then D1 = (1/2)[ -x_M x_N - x_N^2 - x_N(R - r) + x_M x_N + x_M R - x_N r - r R - h^2 ] = (1/2)[ -x_N^2 - x_N(R - r) + x_M R - x_N r - r R - h^2 ].

Now -x_N^2 - h^2 = -R^2? Since R^2 = x_N^2+h^2. So -x_N^2 - h^2 = -R^2. So D1 = (1/2)[ -R^2 - x_N(R - r) + x_M R - x_N r - r R ] = (1/2)[ -R^2 - x_N R + x_N r + x_M R - x_N r - r R ] = (1/2)[ -R^2 - x_N R + x_M R - r R ] = (1/2)[ -R^2 + R(x_M - x_N - r) ] = (1/2) R ( -R + x_M - x_N - r ) = (R/2)( x_M - x_N - r - R ). So D1 = (R/2)( x_M - x_N - r - R ). So D1 = (R/2)( x_M - x_N - r - R ). Good.

Thus we have:

(A-M)·v = D0 = (r/2)( x_M - x_N - r - R ).
(A-N)·v = D1 = (R/2)( x_M - x_N - r - R ).

Let’s denote:

- For a point X, we have circle equation: (x - x_c)^2 + (y - y_c)^2 = R_c^2.
- For B: (0 - x_c)^2 + (-h - y_c)^2 = R_c^2 => x_c^2 + (h + y_c)^2 = R_c^2. (1)
- For E: (t_E p_x - x_c)^2 + (h + t_E β - y_c)^2 = R_c^2. (2)
- For F: (t_F p_x - x_c)^2 + (h + t_F β - y_c)^2 = R_c^2. (3)

Subtract (1) from (2): (t_E p_x - x_c)^2 + (h + t_E β - y_c)^2 - x_c^2 - (h + y_c)^2 = 0.
Expand: t_E^2 p_x^2 - 2 t_E p_x x_c + x_c^2 + (h + t_E β)^2 - 2 (h + t_E β) y_c + y_c^2 - x_c^2 - (h^2 + 2h y_c + y_c^2) = 0 => t_E^2 p_x^2 - 2 t_E p_x x_c + (h + t_E β)^2 - 2 (h + t_E β) y_c - h^2 - 2h y_c = 0. (since x_c^2 and y_c^2 cancel). So:

t_E^2 p_x^2 - 2 t_E p_x x_c + (h^2 + 2h t_E β + t_E^2 β^2) - 2h y_c - 2 t_E β y_c - h^2 - 2h y_c = 0 => Cancel h^2: t_E^2 p_x^2 - 2 t_E p_x x_c + 2h t_E β + t_E^2 β^2 - 2h y_c - 2 t_E β y_c - 2h y_c = 0 => t_E^2 (p_x^2+β^2) - 2 t_E p_x x_c + 2h t_E β - 4h y_c - 2 t_E β y_c = 0. But note that p_x^2+β^2 = L. So:

t_E^2 L - 2 t_E p_x x_c + 2h t_E β - 4h y_c - 2 t_E β y_c = 0. (A)

Similarly, subtract (1) from (3): t_F^2 L - 2 t_F p_x x_c + 2h t_F β - 4h y_c - 2 t_F β y_c = 0. (B)

Now, we have t_E and t_F known. Since t_E = -r K / L, t_F = -R K / L. Let's denote T = K / L. Then t_E = -r T, t_F = -R T. Note that T is negative? K negative, L positive, so T negative. So t_E and t_F positive? -r T: since r>0, T negative, -r T >0. Good.

Now, substitute into (A): (-r T)^2 L - 2 (-r T) p_x x_c + 2h (-r T) β - 4h y_c - 2 (-r T) β y_c = 0 => r^2 T^2 L + 2r T p_x x_c - 2h r T β - 4h y_c + 2r T β y_c = 0. Since T^2 L = (K^2/L^2) * L = K^2 / L. But we might not need that. Let's keep.

Similarly, (B): R^2 T^2 L + 2R T p_x x_c - 2h R T β - 4h y_c + 2R T β y_c = 0.

Now, note that T = K/L. So we have T^2 L = K^2/L.

Thus the equations become:

r^2 (K^2/L) + 2r (K/L) p_x x_c - 2h r (K/L) β - 4h y_c + 2r (K/L) β y_c = 0. (A')
R^2 (K^2/L) + 2R (K/L) p_x x_c - 2h R (K/L) β - 4h y_c + 2R (K/L) β y_c = 0. (B')

Multiply both equations by L/K (assuming K≠0, which holds because circles intersect at two points, so x_M - x_N - r - R ≠ 0? But since r+R > |x_N-x_M|, and x_M - x_N could be negative if x_M < x_N; then left side negative, right side positive, impossible. If x_M > x_N, then x_M - x_N positive; but then r+R > d = x_M-x_N; so x_M - x_N < r+R, so x_M - x_N - r - R <0. So K is negative. So K≠0. So we can divide by K.
